Very common in <a href="#dinghy"><span title="See entry on this page at § dinghy" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">dinghies</span></a>, but also found in some larger boats. A <a href="#daggerboard"><span title="See entry on this page at § daggerboard" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">daggerboard</span></a> serves the same purpose but slides vertically rather than pivoting.</dd>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Centerboard_(PSF).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Centerboard_%28PSF%29.svg/220px-Centerboard_%28PSF%29.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="72" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Centerboard_%28PSF%29.svg/330px-Centerboard_%28PSF%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Centerboard_%28PSF%29.svg/440px-Centerboard_%28PSF%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="745" data-file-height="245" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Centerboard_(PSF).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diagram of the position of a <b><a href="#centerboard"><span title="See entry on this page at § centerboard" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">centerboard</span></a></b> on a boat</div></div></div>
<dt class="glossary" id="chafing" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Chafing_(nautical)" title="Chafing (nautical)">chafing</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Wear on a line or sail caused by constant rubbing against another surface.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="chafing_gear" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">chafing gear</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Material applied to a line or spar to prevent or reduce <a href="#chafing"><span title="See entry on this page at § chafing" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">chafing</span></a>. See <i><a href="#baggywrinkle"><span title="See entry on this page at § baggywrinkle" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">baggywrinkle</span></a></i> and <i><span title="See entry on this page at § puddening" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#puddening" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">puddening</a></span></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnderhill1955108_27-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnderhill1955108-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="chain_locker" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">chain locker</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A space in the forward part of a ship, typically beneath the <a href="#bow"><span title="See entry on this page at § bow" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">bow</span></a> in front of the foremost collision <a href="#bulkhead"><span title="See entry on this page at § bulkhead" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">bulkhead</span></a>, that contains the <a href="#anchor_chain"><span title="See entry on this page at § anchor chain" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">anchor chain</span></a> when the <a href="#anchor"><span title="See entry on this page at § anchor" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">anchor</span></a> is secured for sea.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="chain-shot" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Chain-shot" class="mw-redirect" title="Chain-shot">chain-shot</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Cannonballs linked with short lengths of chain, designed to be especially damaging to <span title="See entry on this page at § rigging" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#rigging" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">rigging</a></span> and <span title="See entry on this page at § mast" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#mast" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">masts</a></span>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="chain_plates" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Chain_plates" class="mw-redirect" title="Chain plates">chain plates</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Iron bars bolted to a ship's side to which the <a href="#deadeye"><span title="See entry on this page at § deadeye" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">deadeyes</span></a> or rigging screws of the lower figging and the <a href="#back-stay"><span title="See entry on this page at § back-stay" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">back-stays</span></a> are bolted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnderhill1955108_27-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnderhill1955108-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="chain-wale" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">chain-wale</dfn></dt>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also <b>channel</b>.</span></p>
<dd class="glossary">A broad, thick plank that projects horizontally from each of a ship's sides abreast a <span title="See entry on this page at § mast" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#mast" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">mast</a></span> (distinguished as the fore, main, or mizzen channel accordingly), serving to extend the base for the <span title="See entry on this page at § shroud" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#shroud" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">shrouds</a></span>, which support the mast.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnderhill1955108_27-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnderhill1955108-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="chains" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Chains_(nautical)" title="Chains (nautical)">chains</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Small platforms built into the sides of a ship to spread the <span title="See entry on this page at § shroud" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#shroud" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">shrouds</a></span> to a more advantageous angle. Also used as a platform for manual <a href="#depth_sounding"><span title="See entry on this page at § depth sounding" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">depth sounding</span></a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="chalupa" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Chalupa_(boat)" title="Chalupa (boat)">chalupa</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;A small boat that functions as a <span title="See entry on this page at § shallop" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#shallop" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">shallop</a></span>, <span title="See entry on this page at § water taxi" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#water_taxi" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">water taxi</a></span>, or <a href="#gondola"><span title="See entry on this page at § gondola" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">gondola</span></a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;In <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>, a small boat used for <a href="#cabotage"><span title="See entry on this page at § cabotage" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">cabotage</span></a>, propelled by either oars or sails. Those equipped with sails have a single mast.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.&#160;&#160;A type of <span title="See entry on this page at § whaling boat" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#whaling_boat" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">whaling boat</a></span> used by the Basques in the mid-16th century in what is now Newfoundland and Labrador.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="channel_fever" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">channel fever</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;The impatient excitement in a ship's crew as the end of a voyage becomes imminent. Characteristics include crew members working harder to get the ship sailing faster, off-watch personnel being on deck to keep track of progress, and everyone being packed and in their shore-going clothes (ready to be paid off) the moment the vessel arrives in port.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;(obsolete usage) A crew member avoiding duties with a feigned illness, usually after leaving port.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="charlie/charley_noble" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Charlie_Noble_(chimney)" title="Charlie Noble (chimney)">Charlie/Charley Noble</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The metal stovepipe chimney from a cook shack on the deck of a ship or from a stove in a <a href="#galley"><span title="See entry on this page at § galley" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">galley</span></a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="chartered_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">chartered ship</dfn></dt>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also <b>charter ship</b>.</span></p>
<dd class="glossary">A term used by the British <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> from the 17th to the 19th centuries for a merchant ship it chartered to make a single, often one-way, voyage between England (later the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom</a>) and ports east of the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a>, a trade over which the company held a strict monopoly. A charter ship during its single voyage was employed in much the same way as what the company called an <a href="#extra_ship"><span title="See entry on this page at § extra ship" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">extra ship</span></a>, though the company usually hired charter ships on special terms and for much shorter periods.<sup id="cite_ref-threedecksshiproles_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-threedecksshiproles-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="charthouse" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">charthouse</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A compartment from which the ship was navigated, especially in the Royal Navy.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="chartplotter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">chartplotter</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An electronic instrument that places the position of the ship (from a <a href="/wiki/GPS_receiver" class="mw-redirect" title="GPS receiver">GPS receiver</a>) onto a digital <span title="See entry on this page at § nautical chart" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#nautical_chart" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">nautical chart</a></span> displayed on a monitor, thereby replacing all manual navigation functions. Chartplotters also display information collected from all shipboard electronic instruments and often directly control autopilots.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="chase_gun" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Chase_gun" title="Chase gun">chase gun</a></dfn></dt><span class="anchor" id="chase_piece"></span><span class="anchor" id="chaser"></span>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also <b>chase piece</b> or <b>chaser</b>.</span></p>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Cannon" title="Cannon">cannon</a> pointing forward or aft, often of longer range than other guns. Those on the bow (<a href="#bow_chaser"><span title="See entry on this page at § bow chaser" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">bow chasers</span></a>) were used to fire upon a ship ahead, while those on the rear (<span title="See entry on this page at § stern chaser" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#stern_chaser" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">stern chasers</a></span>) were used to ward off pursuing vessels. Unlike guns pointing to the side, chasers could be brought to bear in a chase without slowing down the vessel.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="&#39;&#39;chasse-marée&#39;&#39;" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><i><a href="/wiki/Chasse-mar%C3%A9e" title="Chasse-marée">chasse-marée</a></i></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A decked commercial sailing vessel engaged in the transportation of fresh fish directly from fishing grounds to ports in <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a> between the 18th century and around the third quarter of the 19th century. Three-<span title="See entry on this page at § mast" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#mast" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">masted</a></span> <a href="/wiki/Lugger" title="Lugger">luggers</a> replaced the vessels originally serving in this role; the luggers then were replaced successively by <a href="#dundee"><span title="See entry on this page at § dundee" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">dundees</span></a>, <a href="#brig"><span title="See entry on this page at § brig" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">brigs</span></a>, and <a href="/wiki/Schooner" title="Schooner">schooners</a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cheeks" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">cheeks</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;Wooden blocks at the side of a <span title="See entry on this page at § spar" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#spar" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">spar</a></span>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;Flat plates of iron or wood bolted to the <span title="See entry on this page at § masthead" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#masthead" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">masthead</a></span> to form angle supports for the <a href="#cross-trees"><span title="See entry on this page at § cross-trees" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">cross-trees</span></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnderhill1955108_27-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnderhill1955108-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.&#160;&#160;The sides of a block or gun-carriage.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="chief_engineer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Chief_engineer" title="Chief engineer">chief engineer</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The senior engineering officer (abbreviated ChEng).</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="chine" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Chine_(boating)" title="Chine (boating)">chine</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;An angle in the <a href="#hull"><span title="See entry on this page at § hull" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">hull</span></a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;A line formed where the sides of a boat meet the bottom.<sup id="cite_ref-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary_2-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Soft chine is when the two sides join at a shallow angle, and hard chine is when they join at a steep angle.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="chock" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">chock</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A hole or ring attached to the hull to guide a line via that point; an opening in a ship's <a href="#bulwark"><span title="See entry on this page at § bulwark" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">bulwark</span></a>, normally oval in shape, designed to allow mooring lines to be fastened to <a href="#cleat"><span title="See entry on this page at § cleat" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">cleats</span></a> or bits mounted to the ship's deck. See also <i><span title="See entry on this page at § Panama chock" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#Panama_chock" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">Panama chock</a></span></i> and <i><a href="#dutchman&#39;s_chock"><span title="See entry on this page at § Dutchman&#39;s chock" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">Dutchman's chock</span></a></i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="chock-a-block" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">chock-a-block</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Rigging <a href="#block"><span title="See entry on this page at § block" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">blocks</span></a> that are so tight against one another that they cannot be further tightened.<sup id="cite_ref-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary_2-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="chronometer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Marine_chronometer" title="Marine chronometer">chronometer</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A timekeeping device accurate enough to be used aboard a ship to determine <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a> by means of <a href="/wiki/Celestial_navigation" title="Celestial navigation">celestial navigation</a>. The invention of the marine chronometer in the 18th century was a major technical achievement for maritime navigation.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cigarette_boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">cigarette boat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i><a href="#go-fast_boat"><span title="See entry on this page at § go-fast boat" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">go-fast boat</span></a></i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="citadel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Citadel" title="Citadel">citadel</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A fortified safe room on a vessel to take shelter in the event of <span title="See entry on this page at § pirate" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#pirate" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">pirate</a></span> attack. Previously, a fortified room to protect ammunition and machinery from damage.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="civil_red_ensign" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">civil <a href="/wiki/Red_Ensign" title="Red Ensign">Red Ensign</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The British Naval Ensign or flag of the <a href="/wiki/British_Merchant_Navy" class="mw-redirect" title="British Merchant Navy">British Merchant Navy</a>, a red flag with the <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Flag of the United Kingdom">Union Flag</a> in the upper left corner. Colloquially called the "red duster".</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="class" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Ship_class" title="Ship class">class</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;A group of naval ships of the same or similar design. Sometimes used informally to refer to a group of non-naval ships of the same or similar design.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;A standard of construction for merchant vessels, including standards for specific types or specialized capabilities of some types of merchant vessels. A ship meeting the standard is <i>in class</i>, while one not meeting it is <i>out of class</i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="clean_bill_of_health" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">clean bill of health</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A certificate issued by a port indicating that a ship carries no infectious diseases. Also called a <span title="See entry on this page at § pratique" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#pratique" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">pratique</a></span>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="clean_slate" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">clean slate</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">At the <a href="#helm"><span title="See entry on this page at § helm" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">helm</span></a>, the <span title="See entry on this page at § watchstanding" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#watchstanding" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">watchkeeper</a></span> would record details of speed, distances, headings, etc. on a slate. At the beginning of a new watch the slate would be wiped clean.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="clear" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">clear</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To perform <a href="/wiki/Customs" title="Customs">customs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigration</a> legalities prior to leaving port.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cleat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cleat_(nautical)" title="Cleat (nautical)">cleat</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A stationary device used to secure a rope aboard a vessel.<sup id="cite_ref-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary_2-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="clench" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">clench</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A method of fixing together two pieces of wood, usually overlapping planks, by driving a nail through both planks as well as a washer-like rove. The nail is then burred or riveted over to complete the fastening.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="clew" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">clew</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">One of the lower corners of a square sail, or, on a triangular sail, the corner at the end of the <a href="#boom"><span title="See entry on this page at § boom" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">boom</span></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary_2-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="clewlines" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Clewlines_and_buntlines" title="Clewlines and buntlines">clewlines</a></dfn></dt><span class="anchor" id="clew-lines"></span>
<dd class="glossary"><a href="#line"><span title="See entry on this page at § line" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">Lines</span></a> used to truss up the <a href="#clew"><span title="See entry on this page at § clew" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">clews</span></a>, the lower corners of square sails. Used to reduce and stow a barge's <span title="See entry on this page at § topsail" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#topsail" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">topsail</a></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary_2-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="clinker-built" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Clinker_(boat_building)" title="Clinker (boat building)">clinker-built</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A method of constructing <a href="#hull"><span title="See entry on this page at § hull" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">hulls</span></a> that involves overlapping planks and/or plates, much like Viking longships, resulting in speed and flexibility in small boat hulls. Contrast <i><a href="#carvel-built"><span title="See entry on this page at § carvel-built" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">carvel-built</span></a></i>.</dd>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clinker-carvel.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Clinker-carvel.svg/220px-Clinker-carvel.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="111" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Clinker-carvel.svg/330px-Clinker-carvel.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Clinker-carvel.svg/440px-Clinker-carvel.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="549" data-file-height="276" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clinker-carvel.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A comparison of the <b><a href="#clinker-built"><span title="See entry on this page at § clinker-built" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">clinker</span></a></b> and <b><a href="#carvel-built"><span title="See entry on this page at § carvel-built" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">carvel</span></a></b> styles of boat construction</div></div></div>
<dt class="glossary" id="clipper" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Clipper" title="Clipper">clipper</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;A sailing vessel designed primarily for speed. While the <span title="See entry on this page at § square rig" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#square_rig" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">square-rigged</a></span> clipper <a href="#full-rigged_ship"><span title="See entry on this page at § full-rigged ship" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">ships</span></a> of the middle of the 19th century are well known, others, such as <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Clipper" title="Baltimore Clipper">Baltimore Clippers</a> and opium clippers could be rigged differently, often as <span title="See entry on this page at § schooner" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#schooner" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">schooners</a></span>, and a small number of 19th-century clippers were built as <a href="/wiki/Barque" title="Barque">barques</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;A <span title="See entry on this page at § tuna clipper" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#tuna_clipper" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">tuna clipper</a></span>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="close_aboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">close aboard</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Very near (the ship).</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="close-hauled" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Points_of_sail" class="mw-redirect" title="Points of sail">close-hauled</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">(of a vessel) <a href="#beating"><span title="See entry on this page at § beating" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">Beating</span></a> as close to the wind direction as possible.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="clove_hitch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Clove_hitch" title="Clove hitch">clove hitch</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="#bend"><span title="See entry on this page at § bend" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">bend</span></a> used to attach a rope to a post or <a href="#bollard"><span title="See entry on this page at § bollard" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">bollard</span></a>. Also used to finish tying off the <a href="#foresail"><span title="See entry on this page at § foresail" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">foresail</span></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary_2-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="club_hauling" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">club hauling</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A maneuver by which a ship drops one of its <a href="#anchor"><span title="See entry on this page at § anchor" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">anchors</span></a> at high speed in order to turn abruptly. This was sometimes used as a means of obtaining a good firing angle on a pursuing vessel. See <i><a href="#kedge"><span title="See entry on this page at § kedge" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">kedge</span></a></i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="c.o._(or_co)" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">C.O. (or CO)</dfn></dt><span class="anchor" id="CO"></span>
<dd class="glossary">An abbreviation for <a href="#commanding_officer"><span title="See entry on this page at § commanding officer" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">commanding officer</span></a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="coal_hulk" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">coal hulk</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="#hulk"><span title="See entry on this page at § hulk" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">hulk</span></a> used to store coal.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="coal_trimmer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Coal_trimmer" title="Coal trimmer">coal trimmer</a></dfn></dt><span class="anchor" id="trimmer"></span>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also simply <b>trimmer</b>.</span></p>
<dd class="glossary">A person responsible for ensuring that a coal-fired vessel remains in "<span title="See entry on this page at § trim" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#trim" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">trim</a></span>" (evenly balanced) as coal is consumed on a voyage.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="coaling" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Coaling_(ships)" title="Coaling (ships)">coaling</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Loading <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> for use as fuel aboard a <a href="/wiki/Steamship" title="Steamship">steamship</a>. A time-consuming, laborious, and dirty process often undertaken by the entire crew, coaling was a necessity from the early days of steam in the 19th century until the early 20th century, when oil supplanted coal as the fuel of choice for steamships.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="coaming" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Coaming" title="Coaming">coaming</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The raised edge of a hatch, cockpit, or skylight, designed to help keep out water that pools on the surface above.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="coaster" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">coaster</dfn></dt>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also <b>skoot</b>.</span></p>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Coastal_trading_vessel" title="Coastal trading vessel">coastal trading vessel</a>; a shallow-hulled ship used for trade between locations on the same island or continent.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="coble" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Coble" title="Coble">coble</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of open traditional fishing boat with a flat bottom and high bow which developed on the northeast coast of England.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cockbill" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">cockbill</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To angle a square-rigged <span title="See entry on this page at § yard" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#yard" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">yard</a></span> away from the horizontal so that it is out of the way for loading or unloading, or so that the ship may lie alongside another ship without the yards touching.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cockpit" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cockpit_(sailing)" title="Cockpit (sailing)">cockpit</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A seating area (not to be confused with the <a href="#deck"><span title="See entry on this page at § deck" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">deck</span></a>) towards the <span title="See entry on this page at § stern" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#stern" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">stern</a></span> of a small-decked vessel that houses the <span title="See entry on this page at § rudder" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#rudder" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">rudder</a></span> controls.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cofferdam" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cofferdam#Naval_architecture" title="Cofferdam">cofferdam</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An insulating space between two watertight bulkheads or decks within a ship.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cog" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cog_(ship)" title="Cog (ship)">cog</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of sailing ship, with a single <span title="See entry on this page at § mast" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#mast" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">mast</a></span> and a single <a href="/wiki/Square_rig" title="Square rig">square-rigged</a> <span title="See entry on this page at § sail" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#sail" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">sail</a></span> first developed in the 10th century and widely used, particularly in the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a> region, in seagoing trade from the 12th through the 14th centuries. It had a distinctive hull design: the flat bottom was <a href="#carvel-built"><span title="See entry on this page at § carvel-built" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">carvel-built</span></a> and the sides were <a href="#clinker-built"><span title="See entry on this page at § clinker-built" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">clinker-built</span></a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenhill_1995_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenhill_1995-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 225–227">&#58;&#8202;225–227&#8202;</span></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="coign" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Coign_(gunnery)" class="mw-redirect" title="Coign (gunnery)">coign</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A wedge used to assist in the aiming of a cannon; an older form of "quoin".</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="collier" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Collier_(ship)" title="Collier (ship)">collier</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="#bulk_cargo_ship"><span title="See entry on this page at § bulk cargo ship" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">bulk cargo ship</span></a> designed to carry coal, especially such a ship in naval use to supply coal to coal-fired warships.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="combat_loading" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Combat_loading" title="Combat loading">combat loading</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A way of loading a vessel that gives military forces embarked aboard her immediate access to weapons, ammunition, and supplies needed when conducting an amphibious landing. In combat loading, cargo is stowed in such a way that unloading of equipment will match up with the personnel that are landing and in the order they land, so that they have immediate access to the gear they need for combat as soon as they land. Combat loading gives primary consideration to the ease and sequence with which troops, equipment, and supplies can be made ready for combat, sacrificing the more efficient use of cargo space that ship operators seek when loading a ship for the routine transportation of personnel and cargo.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="comber" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Comber_wave" class="mw-redirect" title="Comber wave">comber</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A long, curving wave breaking on the shore.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="come_about" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">come about</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;To <span title="See entry on this page at § tacking" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#tacking" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">tack</a></span>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;To change tack.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.&#160;&#160;To manoeuvre the <a href="#bow"><span title="See entry on this page at § bow" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">bow</span></a> of a sailing vessel across the wind so that the wind changes from one side of the vessel to the other.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.&#160;&#160;To position a vessel with respect to the wind after tacking.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="come_to" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">come to</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To stop a sailing vessel, especially by turning into the wind.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="commanding_officer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">commanding officer</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The officer in command of a warship.  Also called "CO", "captain" (regardless of rank), "skipper", or "the old man".</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="commission" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Ship_commissioning" title="Ship commissioning">commission</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To formally place (a naval vessel) into active service, after which the vessel is said to be <i>in commission</i>. Sometimes used less formally to mean placing a commercial ship into service.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="commissioning_pennant" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Commissioning_pennant" title="Commissioning pennant">commissioning pennant</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Pennon" title="Pennon">pennant</a> flown from the <span title="See entry on this page at § masthead" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#masthead" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">masthead</a></span> of a <a href="/wiki/Warship" title="Warship">warship</a>. Also called a masthead pennant.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="commodore" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">commodore</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;(<a href="/wiki/Commodore_(rank)" title="Commodore (rank)">rank</a>) Prior to 1997, the title used in the Royal Navy for an officer of the rank of <a href="#captain"><span title="See entry on this page at § captain" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">captain</span></a> who was given temporary command of a <span title="See entry on this page at § squadron" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#squadron" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">squadron</a></span>. At the end of the deployment of the squadron, or in the presence of an <a href="#admiral"><span title="See entry on this page at § admiral" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">admiral</span></a>, he would revert to his <i>de facto</i> rank of captain.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;(<a href="/wiki/Commodore_(rank)" title="Commodore (rank)">rank</a>) A military rank used in many navies that is superior to a navy <a href="#captain"><span title="See entry on this page at § captain" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">captain</span></a> but below a <span title="See entry on this page at § rear admiral" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#rear_admiral" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">rear admiral</a></span>. Often equivalent to the rank of "flotilla admiral" or sometimes "counter admiral" in non-English-speaking navies.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.&#160;&#160;(<a href="/wiki/Convoy_commodore" title="Convoy commodore">convoy commodore</a>) A civilian put in charge of the good order of the merchant ships in British convoys during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, but with no authority over naval ships escorting the convoy.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.&#160;&#160;(<a href="/wiki/Commodore_(yacht_club)" class="mw-redirect" title="Commodore (yacht club)">commodore (yacht club)</a>) An officer of a yacht club.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">5.&#160;&#160;(<a href="/wiki/Commodore_(Sea_Scouts)" class="mw-redirect" title="Commodore (Sea Scouts)">Commodore (Sea Scouts)</a>) A position in the <a href="/wiki/Boy_Scouts_of_America" title="Boy Scouts of America">Boy Scouts of America</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Sea_Scouts" class="mw-redirect" title="Sea Scouts">Sea Scouts</a> program.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="communication_tube" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Speaking_tube" title="Speaking tube">communication tube</a></dfn></dt><span class="anchor" id="voice_tube"></span><span class="anchor" id="speaking_tube"></span>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also <b>speaking tube</b> or <b>voice tube</b>.</span></p>
<dd class="glossary">An air-filled tube, usually armored, allowing speech between the <a href="/wiki/Conning_tower" title="Conning tower">conning tower</a> and the below-decks control spaces on a warship.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="companionway" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Companionway" title="Companionway">companionway</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A raised and windowed hatchway in a ship's <a href="#deck"><span title="See entry on this page at § deck" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">deck</span></a>, with a ladder leading below and the hooded entrance-hatch to the main <a href="#cabin"><span title="See entry on this page at § cabin" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">cabins</span></a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="complement" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">complement</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;The number of persons in a ship′s <a href="#crew"><span title="See entry on this page at § crew" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">crew</span></a>, including officers.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;A collective term for all of the persons in a ship′s crew, including officers.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="comprise" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">comprise</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To include or contain. As applied to a naval task force, the listing of all assigned units for a single transient purpose or mission (e.g. "The task force <i>comprises</i> Ship A, Ship B, and Ship C"). "Comprise" means exhaustive inclusion – there are not any other parts to the task force, and each ship has a permanent squadron existence, independent of the task force.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="concrete_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Concrete_ship" title="Concrete ship">concrete ship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel constructed of steel and <a href="/wiki/Ferrocement" title="Ferrocement">ferrocement</a> (a type of <a href="/wiki/Reinforced_concrete" title="Reinforced concrete">reinforced concrete</a>) rather than of more traditional materials, such as steel, iron, or wood.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="conn" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Conn_(nautical)" title="Conn (nautical)">conn</a></dfn></dt>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also <b>con</b>, <b>conne</b>, <b>conde</b>, <b>cunde</b>, or <b>cun</b>.</span></p>
<dd class="glossary">To direct a ship or submarine from a position of command. While performing this duty, an officer is said to <i>have the conn</i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="conning_officer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">conning officer</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An officer on a naval vessel responsible for instructing the <a href="#helmsman"><span title="See entry on this page at § helmsman" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">helmsman</span></a> on the course to steer. While performing this duty, the officer is said to <i>have the conn</i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="conning_tower" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Conning_tower" title="Conning tower">conning tower</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;An armored control tower of an iron or steel warship built between the mid-19th and mid-20th centuries from which the ship was navigated in battle.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;A tower-like structure on the dorsal (topside) surface of a submarine, serving in submarines built before the mid-20th century as a connecting structure between the bridge and pressure hull and housing instruments and controls from which the periscopes were used to direct the submarine and launch <a href="/wiki/Torpedo" title="Torpedo">torpedo</a> attacks. Since the mid-20th century, it has been replaced by the <i><a href="/wiki/Sail_(submarine)" title="Sail (submarine)">sail</a></i> (United States usage) or <i>fin</i> (European and British Commonwealth usage), a structure similar in appearance that no longer plays a role in directing the submarine.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="consort" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Consort_(nautical)" title="Consort (nautical)">consort</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Unpowered <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> vessels, usually a fully loaded <span title="See entry on this page at § schooner" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#schooner" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">schooner</a></span>, <a href="#barge"><span title="See entry on this page at § barge" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">barge</span></a>, or <a href="/wiki/Steamboat" title="Steamboat">steamer</a> barge, towed by a larger steamer that would often tow more than one barge. The consort system was used in the Great Lakes from the 1860s to around 1920.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="constant_bearing,_decreasing_range_(cbdr)" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Constant_bearing,_decreasing_range" title="Constant bearing, decreasing range">constant bearing, decreasing range</a> (CBDR)</dfn></dt><span class="anchor" id="CBDR"></span>
<dd class="glossary">When two boats are approaching each other from any angle and this angle remains the same over time (constant <a href="#bearing"><span title="See entry on this page at § bearing" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">bearing</span></a>) they are on a collision course. Because of the implication of collision, "constant bearing, decreasing range" has come to mean a problem or an obstacle which is incoming.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="container_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Container_ship" title="Container ship">container ship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A cargo ship that carries all of her cargo in truck-size <a href="/wiki/Intermodal_containers" class="mw-redirect" title="Intermodal containers">intermodal containers</a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="convoy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Convoy" title="Convoy">convoy</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A group of ships traveling together for mutual support and protection.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="corinthian" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">corinthian</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An <a href="/wiki/Amateur_sports" title="Amateur sports">amateur</a> <a href="/wiki/Yachting" title="Yachting">yachter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="corrector" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Corrector" title="Corrector">corrector</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A device used to correct the ship's compass, e.g. by counteracting errors due to the magnetic effects of a steel hull.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="corsair" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">corsair</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;A <a href="/wiki/French_corsairs" title="French corsairs">French privateer</a>, especially one from the port of <a href="/wiki/St-Malo" class="mw-redirect" title="St-Malo">St-Malo</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;Any privateer or pirate.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.&#160;&#160;A ship used by privateers or pirates, especially of French nationality.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.&#160;&#160;(<a href="/wiki/Corsair_(dinghy)" title="Corsair (dinghy)">corsair (dinghy)</a>) A class of 16-foot (4.9-metre) three-handed sailing <a href="#dinghy"><span title="See entry on this page at § dinghy" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">dinghy</span></a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="corvette" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Corvette" title="Corvette">corvette</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;A flush-decked sailing warship of the 17th, 18th, and 19th centuries having a single tier of guns, ranked next below a <a href="#frigate"><span title="See entry on this page at § frigate" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">frigate</span></a>. In the US Navy, it is referred to as a <a href="/wiki/Sloop-of-war" title="Sloop-of-war">sloop-of-war</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;A lightly armed and armored warship of the 20th and 21st centuries, smaller than a frigate, and capable of trans-oceanic duty.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cotchel" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">cotchel</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A partial load.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBenhamFinchKershaw1986186_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBenhamFinchKershaw1986186-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cottonclad" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cottonclad_warship" title="Cottonclad warship">cottonclad</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A steam-powered wooden warship protected from enemy fire by bales of <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a> lining its sides, most commonly associated with some of the warships employed by the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate States of America</a> during the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> (1861–1865).</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="counter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">counter</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The part of the <span title="See entry on this page at § stern" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#stern" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">stern</a></span> above the <span title="See entry on this page at § waterline" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#waterline" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">waterline</a></span> that extends beyond the <span title="See entry on this page at § rudder" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#rudder" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">rudder</a></span> stock culminating in a small <span title="See entry on this page at § transom" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#transom" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">transom</a></span>. A long counter increases the waterline length when the boat is <a href="#heeled"><span title="See entry on this page at § heeled" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">heeled</span></a>, so increasing hull speed. See also <i><span title="See entry on this page at § truncated counter" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#truncated_counter" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">truncated counter</a></span></i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="counterflood" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">counterflood</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To deliberately flood compartments on the opposite side from already flooded ones. Usually done to reduce a <a href="/wiki/Angle_of_list" title="Angle of list">list</a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="country_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">country ship</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A term used by the British <a href="/wiki/East_India_Company" title="East India Company">East India Company</a> from the seventeenth to the nineteenth century for a merchant ship owned by local owners east of the <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a> which traded within that area and gathered cargoes for shipment west of the Cape to England (later the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom</a>) by the company's "chartered ships" (<i>q.v.</i>),"extra ships" (<i>q.v.</i>), and "regular ships" (<i>q.v.</i>). "Country ships" were strictly prohibited from trading west of the Cape, which would violate the company's strict monopoly on that trade.<sup id="cite_ref-threedecksshiproles_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-threedecksshiproles-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Country ships were also important in the opium trade from India into China until supplanted by the faster opium clipper.<sup id="cite_ref-Trocki_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Trocki-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 51">&#58;&#8202;51&#8202;</span></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="course" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">course</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;The direction in which a vessel is being steered, usually given in degrees.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;The lowest square sail on a square rigged mast, except where that mast is the mizzen&#160;&#8211;&#32;in which case the name <a href="#cro&#39;jack"><span title="See entry on this page at § cro&#39;jack" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">cro'jack</span></a> (cross-jack) or mizzen-sail is used.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBennett20058_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBennett20058-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cowl" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">cowl</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;A ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s ventilator with a bell-shaped top that can be swiveled to catch the wind and force it below.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;A vertical projection of a ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s funnel that directs the smoke away from the <a href="#bridge"><span title="See entry on this page at § bridge" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">bridge</span></a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="coxswain" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Coxswain" title="Coxswain">coxswain</a></dfn></dt><span class="anchor" id="cockswain"></span>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also <b>cockswain</b>.</span></p>
<dd class="glossary">The <a href="#helmsman"><span title="See entry on this page at § helmsman" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">helmsman</span></a> or crew member in command of a boat.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="crab" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">crab</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A winch used for raising the <a href="#leeboard"><span title="See entry on this page at § leeboard" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">leeboard</span></a>, with a barrel for pulling in the <span title="See entry on this page at § staysail" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#staysail" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">staysail</a></span> sheets.<sup id="cite_ref-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary_2-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="crabber" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">crabber</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A fishing vessel rigged for <a href="/wiki/Crab_fishing" class="mw-redirect" title="Crab fishing">crab fishing</a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="crane_vessel_or_crane_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Crane_vessel" title="Crane vessel">crane vessel</a> or crane ship</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship with a crane and specialized for lifting heavy loads.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cranse_iron" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">cranse iron</dfn></dt>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also <b>crance</b>, <b>crans</b>, or <b>cranze iron</b>.</span></p>
<dd class="glossary">The metal fitting mounted at the end of a <a href="#bowsprit"><span title="See entry on this page at § bowsprit" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">bowsprit</span></a> to which the <a href="#forestay"><span title="See entry on this page at § forestay" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">forestay</span></a> (or jibstay), <a href="#bobstay"><span title="See entry on this page at § bobstay" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">bobstay</span></a>, and bowsprit shrouds are attached. It is also where the tack of the outermost <a href="#headsail"><span title="See entry on this page at § headsail" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">headsail</span></a> is fastened.<sup id="cite_ref-PBO_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBO-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="crash_rescue_boat_or_crash_boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Crash_rescue_boat" title="Crash rescue boat">crash rescue boat</a> or crash boat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A term used in the United States to describe military high-speed offshore rescue boats, similar in size and performance to motor torpedo boats, used to rescue pilots and aircrews of crashed aircraft.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="crazy_ivan" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Crazy Ivan</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">US Navy slang for a maneuver in which a submerged Soviet or Russian submarine suddenly turns 180 degrees or through 360 degrees to detect submarines following it.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="crew" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Crew" title="Crew">crew</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;On warships and merchant ships, all of those members of a <span title="See entry on this page at § ship&#39;s company" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#ship&#39;s_company" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">ship's company</a></span> who are not officers.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;On leisure vessels with no formal chain of command, all of those persons who are not the skipper or passengers.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="crew_boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Crew_boat" title="Crew boat">crew boat</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel specialized for the transportation of offshore support personnel and cargo to and from offshore installations such as oil platforms, drilling rigs, drill ships, dive ships, and wind farms. Also known as a <i>fast support vessel</i> or <i>fast supply vessel</i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="crew_management" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Crew_management" title="Crew management">crew management</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The services rendered by specialised shipping companies to manage the human resources and manning of all types of vessels, including recruitment, deployment to vessel, scheduling, and training, as well as the ongoing management and administrative duties of seafarers, such as payroll, travel arrangements, insurance and health schemes, overall career development, and day-to-day welfare. Also known as <i>crewing</i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cringle" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">cringle</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A loop of rope, usually at the corners of a sail, for fixing the sail to a <span title="See entry on this page at § spar" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#spar" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">spar</a></span>. They are often reinforced with a metal eye.<sup id="cite_ref-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary_2-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cro&#39;jack" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">cro'jack</dfn></dt><span class="anchor" id="crossjack"></span>
<dd class="glossary">The square sail set on the lower mizzen yard of a square-rigged ship. Many full-rigged ships would not set a sail in this position, as it would be interfered with by the <span title="See entry on this page at § spanker" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#spanker" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">spanker</a></span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUnderhill194612_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUnderhill194612-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="crosstrees" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Crosstrees" title="Crosstrees">crosstrees</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Two horizontal struts at the upper ends of the <span title="See entry on this page at § topmast" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#topmast" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">topmasts</a></span> of sailboats, used to anchor the shrouds from the <span title="See entry on this page at § topgallant" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#topgallant" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">topgallant</a></span> mast. Lateral spreaders for the topmast shrouds (standing back stays).<sup id="cite_ref-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary_2-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="crow&#39;s_nest" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Crow%27s_nest" title="Crow&#39;s nest">crow's nest</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <span title="See entry on this page at § masthead" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#masthead" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">masthead</a></span> constructed with sides and sometimes a roof to shelter the lookouts from the weather, generally by whaling vessels. The term has also become generic for what is properly called a masthead.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cruise_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cruise_ship" title="Cruise ship">cruise ship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A passenger ship used for pleasure voyages, where the voyage itself and the ship's amenities are part of the experience, as well as the different destinations along the way. Transportation is not the prime purpose, as cruise ships operate mostly on routes that return passengers to their originating port. A cruise ship contrasts with a <i>passenger liner</i>, which is a passenger ship that provides a scheduled service between published ports primarily as a mode of transportation. Large, prestigious passenger ships used for either purpose are sometimes called <i>ocean liners</i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cruiser" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cruiser" title="Cruiser">cruiser</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;From the mid-19th century to the mid-20th century, a classification for a wide variety of gun- and sometimes <a href="/wiki/Torpedo" title="Torpedo">torpedo</a>-armed warships, usually but not always armored, intended for independent scouting, raiding, or commerce protection; some were designed also to provide direct support to a battle fleet. Cruisers carried out functions performed previously by the cruising ships (sailing frigates and sloops-of-war) of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Sail" title="Age of Sail">Age of Sail</a>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;From the early to the mid-20th century, a type of armored warship with varying armament and of various sizes, but always smaller than a <a href="/wiki/Battleship" title="Battleship">battleship</a> and larger than a <a href="/wiki/Destroyer" title="Destroyer">destroyer</a>, capable of both direct support of a battle fleet and of independent operations, armed with guns and sometimes torpedoes.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.&#160;&#160;After the mid-20th century, various types of warships of intermediate size armed with guided missiles and sometimes guns, intended for air defense of <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carriers</a> and associated task forces or for anti-ship missile attacks against such forces; virtually indistinguishable from large destroyers since the late 20th century.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.&#160;&#160;A yacht with a cabin(s) containing the facilities for living aboard, thus capable of making <span title="See entry on this page at § voyages" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#voyages" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">voyages</a></span>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="crutches" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">crutches</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Metal Y-shaped pins used to fix oars while rowing.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cuddy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cuddy_(cabin)" title="Cuddy (cabin)">cuddy</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small <a href="#cabin"><span title="See entry on this page at § cabin" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">cabin</span></a> in a boat; a cabin, for the use of the captain, in the after part of a sailing ship under the <span title="See entry on this page at § poop deck" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#poop_deck" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">poop deck</a></span>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cunningham" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cunningham_(sailing)" title="Cunningham (sailing)">Cunningham</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A line invented by <a href="/wiki/Briggs_Cunningham" title="Briggs Cunningham">Briggs Cunningham</a>, used to control the shape of a sail.<sup id="cite_ref-BCuk_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BCuk-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cunt_splice_or_cut_splice" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Rope_splicing#Types_of_splices" title="Rope splicing">cunt splice</a> or cut splice</dfn></dt><span class="anchor" id="Cut_splice"></span>
<dd class="glossary">A join between two lines, similar to an eye-splice, where each rope end is joined to the other a short distance along, making an opening that closes under tension.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cuntline" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">cuntline</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The "valley" between the strands of a rope or cable. Before serving a section of laid rope, e.g. to protect it from <a href="#chafing"><span title="See entry on this page at § chafing" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">chafing</span></a>, it may be "wormed" by laying yarns in the cuntlines, giving that section an even cylindrical shape.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cut_and_run" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">cut and run</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">When wanting to make a quick escape, a ship might cut lashings to sails or cables for anchors, causing damage to the rigging, or losing an anchor, but shortening the time needed to <span title="See entry on this page at § make ready" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#make_ready" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">make ready</a></span> by bypassing the proper procedures.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cut_of_his_jib" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">cut of his jib</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The "cut" of a sail refers to its shape. Since this would often vary between ships, it could be used both to identify a familiar vessel at a distance, and to judge the possible sailing qualities of an unknown one. During the 16th and 17th centuries, the ships of different nations used visually distinctive types of <a href="#jib"><span title="See entry on this page at § jib" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">jibs</span></a> that could be determined at a distance, providing an easy way to determine friend from foe.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Also used figuratively of people.<sup id="cite_ref-Jeans_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeans-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cutter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cutter_(boat)" title="Cutter (boat)">cutter</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;A sailing vessel defined by its rig. In European waters this is a single-masted <a href="#fore-and-aft_rigging"><span title="See entry on this page at § fore-and-aft rigging" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">fore and aft rig</span></a> with two or more <a href="#headsail"><span title="See entry on this page at § headsail" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">headsails</span></a> In North American waters, the definition also considers whether or not the bowsprit is permanently fixed and also takes into account the position of the mast. A standing (permanently fixed) bowsprit and a forward mast position, but with two or more headsails would be classed as a sloop in the North American definition. A running bowsprit, a forestay (carrying a staysail) that is fixed to the stemhead, a jib that is set flying and a mast position that is more aft is a cutter.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;A type of <span title="See entry on this page at § ship&#39;s boat" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#ship&#39;s_boat" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">ship's boat</a></span> powered by sail or oars, though more optimised for sail than many types of ship's boat. </dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.&#160;&#160;A small- or medium-sized vessel used by governmental agencies or law enforcement in the exercise of official authority, such as harbor <a href="/wiki/Maritime_pilot" title="Maritime pilot">pilots'</a> cutters, <a href="/wiki/US_Coast_Guard" class="mw-redirect" title="US Coast Guard">US Coast Guard</a> cutters, and <a href="/wiki/UK_Border_Agency" title="UK Border Agency">UK Border Agency</a> cutters.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">4.&#160;&#160;A type of decked sailing vessel originating in the early 18th century designed for speed. Originally this was just a hull type, but came to refer to the rig, which was single masted with both fore and aft and square sails. Very large sail areas were available for light winds. Many were used as small warships.</dd>
