<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cutter_rigging.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Cutter_rigging.svg/220px-Cutter_rigging.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="251" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Cutter_rigging.svg/330px-Cutter_rigging.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Cutter_rigging.svg/440px-Cutter_rigging.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="836" data-file-height="955" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cutter_rigging.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical <b><a href="#cutter"><span title="See entry on this page at § cutter" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">cutter</span></a></b> rigging: one mast bearing a <a href="/wiki/Fore-and-aft_rig" title="Fore-and-aft rig">fore-and-aft rigged</a> <span title="See entry on this page at § mainsail" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#mainsail" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">mainsail</a></span> (A) and <a href="#foresail"><span title="See entry on this page at § foresail" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">foresail</span></a> (<span title="See entry on this page at § staysail" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#staysail" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">forestaysail</a></span>) (B), a <a href="#bowsprit"><span title="See entry on this page at § bowsprit" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">bowsprit</span></a> (C), a <a href="#jib"><span title="See entry on this page at § jib" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">jib</span></a> (D), and a <a href="#flying_jib"><span title="See entry on this page at § flying jib" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">flying jib</span></a> (E)</div></div></div>
<dt class="glossary" id="cutting_out" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Cutting_out" class="mw-redirect" title="Cutting out">cutting out</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A surprise attack by small boats, often at night, against an anchored vessel in which the small-boat crews boarded and captured or destroyed the target vessel. Cutting out became a popular tactic in the latter part of the 18th century and saw extensive use during the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="cutwater" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">cutwater</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The forward curve of the <span title="See entry on this page at § stem" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#stem" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">stem</a></span> of a ship.</dd>

</dl>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="D">D</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(A-L)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: D">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl class="glossary">
<dt class="glossary" id="daggerboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Daggerboard" title="Daggerboard">daggerboard</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of light <a href="#centerboard"><span title="See entry on this page at § centerboard" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">centerboard</span></a> that is lifted vertically; sometimes in pairs, with the <a href="#leeward"><span title="See entry on this page at § leeward" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">leeward</span></a> one lowered when <a href="#beating"><span title="See entry on this page at § beating" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">beating</span></a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dan_or_dan-buoy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">dan or dan-buoy</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A temporary marker <a href="#buoy"><span title="See entry on this page at § buoy" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">buoy</span></a> consisting of a long pole with flag and/or light at the top and, lower down, a float and a ballast weight to make it float vertically. May be used with or without an anchor to attach it to the sea bed. In naval use often marks a swept channel created by minesweeping. In other uses may mark fishing equipment (nets or pots), an anchor, or, most commonly, is attached to a lifebuoy to throw into the sea to mark the position of a <span title="See entry on this page at § man overboard" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#man_overboard" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">man overboard</a></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-PBO_11-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBO-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalmer1975_3-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalmer1975-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dandy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">dandy</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;A rig with a small <span title="See entry on this page at § mizzen" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#mizzen" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">mizzen</a></span> <a href="#abaft"><span title="See entry on this page at § abaft" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">abaft</span></a> the <span title="See entry on this page at § steering post" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#steering_post" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">steering post</a></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary_2-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;In British usage, another name for a <span title="See entry on this page at § yawl" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#yawl" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">yawl</a></span>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.&#160;&#160;In British usage, a small after-sail on a yawl.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="danlayer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Danlayer" title="Danlayer">danlayer</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="/wiki/Minelaying" class="mw-redirect" title="Minelaying">mine warfare</a> vessel, usually a small <span title="See entry on this page at § trawler" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#trawler" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">trawler</a></span>, fitted for laying <a href="#dan"><span title="See entry on this page at § dan" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">dan</span></a>s. Danlayers served as a part of <span title="See entry on this page at § minesweeping" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#minesweeping" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">minesweeping</a></span> <a href="#flotilla"><span title="See entry on this page at § flotilla" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">flotilla</span></a>s during and immediately after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> (1939–1945).</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dart" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">dart</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To run dart; to run dead before the wind.<sup id="cite_ref-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary_2-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="davit" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Davit" title="Davit">davit</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;A spar formerly used on board ships as a crane to hoist the flukes of the <a href="#anchor"><span title="See entry on this page at § anchor" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">anchor</span></a> to the top of the <a href="#bow"><span title="See entry on this page at § bow" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">bow</span></a> without injuring the sides of the ship.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;A crane, often working in pairs and usually made of steel, used to lower things over the side of a ship, including <a href="#lifeboat"><span title="See entry on this page at § lifeboat" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">lifeboats</span></a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="davy_jones&#39;_locker" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Davy_Jones%27_Locker" class="mw-redirect" title="Davy Jones&#39; Locker">Davy Jones' Locker</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An idiom for the bottom of the sea.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="day_beacon" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Day_beacon" title="Day beacon">day beacon</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An unlighted fixed structure equipped with a <a href="#dayboard"><span title="See entry on this page at § dayboard" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">dayboard</span></a> for daytime identification.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="day-blink" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">day-blink</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The moment at dawn where, from some point on the <span title="See entry on this page at § mast" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#mast" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">mast</a></span>, a lookout can see above low-lying mist around the ship.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dayboard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">dayboard</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The daytime identifier of an <a href="#aid_to_navigation"><span title="See entry on this page at § aid to navigation" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">aid to navigation</span></a> presenting one of several standard shapes (square, triangle, or rectangle) and colors (red, green, white, orange, yellow, or black).</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="day_boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">day boat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160; A sailing boat without overnight accommodation, sometimes with a small cabin, used for pleasure sailing. Typically around 20 feet in length<sup id="cite_ref-PBO_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBO-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;(United States): a steamboat built for daytime service; as opposed to a <span title="See entry on this page at § night boat" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#night_boat" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">night boat</a></span>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dead_ahead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">dead ahead</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Exactly ahead; directly ahead; directly in front.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dead_horse" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">dead horse</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Debt owed for advanced pay.<sup id="cite_ref-NHHC_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHHC-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The "<a href="/wiki/Flogging_a_dead_horse#Earlier_related_terms" title="Flogging a dead horse">flogging a dead horse</a>" ceremony at sea celebrated discharge of the debt.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dead_in_the_water" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">dead in the water</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Not moving (used only when a vessel is afloat and neither tied up nor anchored). The term is abbreviated to DIW by the <a href="/wiki/US_Navy" class="mw-redirect" title="US Navy">US Navy</a>. It is often used to indicate that a pirate or drug runner vessel has been immobilised.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dead_run" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">dead run</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i><span title="See entry on this page at § running" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#running" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">running</a></span></i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dead_slow" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">dead slow</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i><span title="See entry on this page at § steerageway" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#steerageway" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">steerageway</a></span></i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dead_wake" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">dead wake</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The trail of a fading disturbance in the water. See also <i><span title="See entry on this page at § wake" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#wake" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">wake</a></span></i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="deadeye" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Deadeye" title="Deadeye">deadeye</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A wooden block with three holes (but no pulleys) spliced to a <span title="See entry on this page at § shroud" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#shroud" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">shroud</a></span>. It adjusts the tension in the <span title="See entry on this page at § standing rigging" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#standing_rigging" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">standing rigging</a></span> of large sailing vessels, by lacing through the holes with a lanyard to the deck. It performs the same job as a <span title="See entry on this page at § turnbuckle" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#turnbuckle" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">turnbuckle</a></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary_2-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="deadhead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">deadhead</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <span title="See entry on this page at § snag" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#snag" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">snag</a></span>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="deadlight" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">deadlight</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A strong shutter fitted over a porthole or other opening that can be closed in bad weather.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dead_reckoning" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dead_reckoning" title="Dead reckoning">dead reckoning</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A method of navigation that estimates a ship's position from the distance run measured by the log and the course steered. If corrections for factors such as tide and leeway are then made, this provides an estimated position.<sup id="cite_ref-Bill_Anderson_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bill_Anderson-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Dead reckoning contrasts with fixing a position with <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_navigation" class="mw-redirect" title="Astronomical navigation">astronomical navigation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Satellite_navigation" title="Satellite navigation">satellite navigation</a>. Some sources consider that a dead reckoning position does include adjustments for wind and tide, so care is needed in interpretation of this term.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="deadrise" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">deadrise</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The angle of the <a href="#hull"><span title="See entry on this page at § hull" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">hull</span></a> surface, relative to horizontal, either side of the <a href="/wiki/Keel" title="Keel">keel</a> and on a line drawn towards the turn of the <a href="#bilge"><span title="See entry on this page at § bilge" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">bilge</span></a>. Without any other qualifier, it is taken at the midships cross-section of the hull. This can be expressed in degrees or sometimes as a vertical linear measure (such as inches) at a standard distance from the keel. A hull with a lot of deadrise has an obvious "V" shape to the bottom of the hull, whereas no deadrise denotes a flat-bottomed hull. It is usually taken to be one of several measures of the "sharpness" of a hull. It can also be referred to as the "rise of <a href="#floor"><span title="See entry on this page at § floor" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">floor</span></a>".<sup id="cite_ref-Gardiner_1993_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gardiner_1993-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="deadwood" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">deadwood</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In a traditional wooden hull, blocks of timber on the top of the keel that form the shape of the hull where its section is too narrow for the method of construction employed elsewhere. It is often used forward of the <span title="See entry on this page at § sternpost" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#sternpost" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">sternpost</a></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESteffy2013_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESteffy2013-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="death_roll" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Death_roll" title="Death roll">death roll</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">In a <a href="/wiki/Keel" title="Keel">keel</a> boat, the act of <a href="#broach"><span title="See entry on this page at § broach" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">broaching</span></a> to <span title="See entry on this page at § windward" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#windward" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">windward</a></span>, putting the <span title="See entry on this page at § spinnaker pole" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#spinnaker_pole" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">spinnaker pole</a></span> into the water and causing a crash-<a href="#gybe"><span title="See entry on this page at § gybe" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">gybe</span></a> of the <a href="#boom"><span title="See entry on this page at § boom" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">boom</span></a> and <span title="See entry on this page at § mainsail" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#mainsail" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">mainsail</a></span>, which sweep across the deck and plunge down into the water. During a death roll, the boat <span title="See entry on this page at § roll" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#roll" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">rolls</a></span> from side to side, becoming gradually more unstable until either it <a href="#capsize"><span title="See entry on this page at § capsize" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">capsizes</span></a> or the skipper reacts correctly to prevent it.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="debarcation_or_disembarkation" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Debark_(ship)" class="mw-redirect" title="Debark (ship)">debarcation</a> or disembarkation</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The process of leaving a ship or aircraft, or removing goods from a ship or aircraft.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="debunk" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">debunk</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The process of removing fuel from a vessel. After a shipwreck, a "debunkering" operation will be performed in an effort to minimize damage and protect the environment from fuel spills.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="deck" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Deck_(ship)" title="Deck (ship)">deck</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;The top of a ship or vessel; the surface that is removed to accommodate the seating area.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;Any of the structures forming the approximately horizontal surfaces in the ship's general structure. Unlike flats, they are a structural part of the ship.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="deck_hand" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">deck hand</dfn></dt><span class="anchor" id="decky"></span>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also <b>decky</b>.</span></p>
<dd class="glossary">A person whose job involves aiding the deck supervisor in (un)mooring, anchoring, maintenance, and general evolutions on deck.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="deck_supervisor" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">deck supervisor</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The person in charge of all evolutions and maintenance on deck; sometimes split into two groups: forward deck supervisor and aft deck supervisor.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="deckhead" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Deckhead" title="Deckhead">deckhead</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The underside of the <a href="#deck"><span title="See entry on this page at § deck" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">deck</span></a> above. The inside of the boat is normally paneled over to hide the structure, pipes, electrical wires. It can be in thin wood planks, often covered with a vinyl lining, or in thin PVC or now even in fiberglass planks.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="deckhouse" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">deckhouse</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A <a href="#cabin"><span title="See entry on this page at § cabin" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">cabin</span></a> that protrudes above a ship's <a href="#deck"><span title="See entry on this page at § deck" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">deck</span></a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="decks_awash" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">decks awash</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A situation in which the <a href="#deck"><span title="See entry on this page at § deck" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">deck</span></a> of the vessel is partially or wholly submerged, possibly as a result of excessive <a href="#list"><span title="See entry on this page at § list" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">listing</span></a> or a loss of buoyancy.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="decommission" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Ship_decommissioning" class="mw-redirect" title="Ship decommissioning">decommission</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To formally take (a naval vessel) out of active service, after which the vessel is said to be <i>out of commission</i> or <i>decommissioned</i>. Sometimes used less formally to mean taking a commercial ship out of service.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="degaussing" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Degaussing" title="Degaussing">degaussing</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A process to reduce a warship's magnetic signature.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="demurrage" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Demurrage" title="Demurrage">demurrage</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A fee paid by a charter party to a shipowner if the time taken to load or unload a vessel exceeds the <a href="#laytime"><span title="See entry on this page at § laytime" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">laytime</span></a>&#160;&#8211;&#32;the amount of time stipulated for loading or unloading&#160;&#8211;&#32;specified in a <a href="/wiki/Voyage_charter" class="mw-redirect" title="Voyage charter">voyage charter</a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="depot_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Depot_ship" title="Depot ship">depot ship</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A ship that acts as a mobile or fixed base for other ships and submarines or that supports a naval base.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="depth_of_hold" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">depth of hold</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The distance between the underside of the main <a href="#deck"><span title="See entry on this page at § deck" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">deck</span></a> (or its supporting beams) and the top of the limber boards (the part of the ceiling that lies alongside the <a href="#keelson"><span title="See entry on this page at § keelson" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">keelson</span></a>), measured at the middle frame. It is one of the key measurements in working out the measurement tonnage in most systems.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESteffy2013_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESteffy2013-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="derrick" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Derrick" title="Derrick">derrick</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A lifting device composed of one <span title="See entry on this page at § mast" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#mast" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">mast</a></span> or pole and a <a href="#boom"><span title="See entry on this page at § boom" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">boom</span></a> or <a href="#jib"><span title="See entry on this page at § jib" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">jib</span></a> hinged freely at the bottom.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="despatch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Demurrage" title="Demurrage">despatch</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A fee paid by a shipowner to a charter party if the time taken to load or unload a vessel is less than the <a href="#laytime"><span title="See entry on this page at § laytime" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">laytime</span></a>&#160;&#8211;&#32;the amount of time stipulated for loading or unloading&#160;&#8211;&#32;specified in a <a href="/wiki/Voyage_charter" class="mw-redirect" title="Voyage charter">voyage charter</a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="despatch_boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">despatch boat</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An alternate spelling of <a href="#dispatch_boat"><span title="See entry on this page at § dispatch boat" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">dispatch boat</span></a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="destroyer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Destroyer" title="Destroyer">destroyer</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of fast and maneuverable small warship introduced in the 1890s to protect <a href="#capital_ship"><span title="See entry on this page at § capital ship" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">capital ships</span></a> from <a href="/wiki/Torpedo_boat" title="Torpedo boat">torpedo boat</a> attack, and since increased in size and capabilities to become a long-endurance warship intended to escort larger vessels in a fleet, convoy, or battle group and defend them against submarines, surface ships, aircraft, or missiles. Originally <i>torpedo boat destroyer</i>. In US Navy slang, also called a <i>tin can</i> or <i>small boy</i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="destroyer_depot_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">destroyer depot ship</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i><a href="#destroyer_tender"><span title="See entry on this page at § destroyer tender" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">destroyer tender</span></a></i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="destroyer_escort" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Destroyer_escort" title="Destroyer escort">destroyer escort</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A US Navy term for a smaller, lightly armed warship built in large numbers during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> (and in smaller numbers thereafter), cheaper, slower, and less-well-armed than a <a href="#destroyer"><span title="See entry on this page at § destroyer" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">destroyer</span></a> but larger and more heavily armed than a <a href="#corvette"><span title="See entry on this page at § corvette" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">corvette</span></a> and designed to escort convoys of merchant ships or naval auxiliaries or second-line naval forces. Employed primarily for anti-submarine warfare, but also used to provide some protection against aircraft and smaller surface ships. Generally known as <a href="#frigate"><span title="See entry on this page at § frigate" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">frigates</span></a> in other navies, and designated as such in the US Navy as well by the 1970s.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="destroyer_leader" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Destroyer_leader" title="Destroyer leader">destroyer leader</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A large <a href="#destroyer"><span title="See entry on this page at § destroyer" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">destroyer</span></a> suitable for commanding a <a href="#flotilla"><span title="See entry on this page at § flotilla" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">flotilla</span></a> of destroyers or other small warships; a type of <a href="#flotilla_leader"><span title="See entry on this page at § flotilla leader" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">flotilla leader</span></a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="destroyer_tender" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Destroyer_tender" title="Destroyer tender">destroyer tender</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A naval <a href="#auxiliary_ship"><span title="See entry on this page at § auxiliary ship" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">auxiliary ship</span></a> designed to provide maintenance support to a flotilla of destroyers or other small warships. Known in British English as a <i>destroyer depot ship</i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="devil_seam" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">devil seam</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The devil was possibly a slang term for the garboard seam, hence "<a href="#between_the_devil_and_the_deep_blue_sea"><span title="See entry on this page at § between the devil and the deep blue sea" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">between the devil and the deep blue sea</span></a>" being an allusion to keel hauling, but a more popular version seems to be the seam between the waterway and the <span title="See entry on this page at § stanchion" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#stanchion" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">stanchions</a></span>, which would be difficult to get at, requiring a cranked caulking iron, and a restricted swing of the caulking mallet.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="devil_to_pay" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">devil to pay</dfn></dt>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also <b>devil to pay, and no pitch hot</b>.</span></p>
<dd class="glossary">"Paying" the devil is sealing the <a href="#devil_seam"><span title="See entry on this page at § devil seam" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">devil seam</span></a>. It is a difficult and unpleasant job (with no resources) because of the shape of the seam (up against the stanchions) or if the devil refers to the garboard seam, it must be done with the ship slipped or <a href="#careening"><span title="See entry on this page at § careening" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">careened</span></a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="devil&#39;s_claw" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Anchor_windlass#Devil&#39;s_claw" title="Anchor windlass">devil's claw</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of chain stopper often used to secure an anchor in its <a href="#hawsepipe"><span title="See entry on this page at § hawsepipe" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">hawsepipe</span></a>. Consists of a two-pronged hook that fits over a link of chain, a <span title="See entry on this page at § turnbuckle" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#turnbuckle" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">turnbuckle</a></span> and a short chain fastened to a strong point.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dhow" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dhow" title="Dhow">dhow</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The generic name of a number of traditional sailing vessels with one or more masts with <a href="#lateen_sail"><span title="See entry on this page at § lateen sail" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">lateen sails</span></a> used in the Red Sea and Indian Ocean region, typically weighing 300 to 500 tons, with a long, thin hull. They are trading vessels primarily used to carry heavy items, like fruit, fresh water, or merchandise. Crews vary from about thirty to around twelve, depending on the size of the vessel.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="diamonds" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">diamonds</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Glass prisms that were laid between the wooden deck planks to allow natural light below were referred to as diamonds due to the sparkle they gave off in the sunlight.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dinghy" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dinghy" title="Dinghy">dinghy</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;A type of small boat, often carried or towed as a <span title="See entry on this page at § ship&#39;s boat" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#ship&#39;s_boat" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">ship's boat</a></span> by a larger vessel.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;A small racing yacht or recreational open sailing boat, often used for beginner training rather than sailing full-sized yachts.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.&#160;&#160;Utility dinghies are usually rowboats or have an outboard motor, but some are rigged for sailing.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dipping_the_eye" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">dipping the eye</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A method of attaching more than one <a href="#hawser"><span title="See entry on this page at § hawser" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">hawser</span></a> to a single <a href="#bollard"><span title="See entry on this page at § bollard" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">bollard</span></a>, so that each can be lifted off without disturbing the other(s). The second hawser is passed under the first, then up through the eye of the first (hence the name), before being secured over the bollard.</dd>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Multiple_mooring_correct.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Multiple_mooring_correct.png/170px-Multiple_mooring_correct.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Multiple_mooring_correct.png/255px-Multiple_mooring_correct.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Multiple_mooring_correct.png/340px-Multiple_mooring_correct.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Multiple_mooring_correct.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dipping the eye</div></div></div>
<dt class="glossary" id="dipping_the_flag" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Flag_dipping" title="Flag dipping">dipping the flag</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A method of rendering honors at sea by lowering and raising a ship's flag.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="directional_light" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">directional light</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A light illuminating a sector or very narrow angle and intended to mark a direction to be followed.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dispatch_boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dispatch_boat" title="Dispatch boat">dispatch boat</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A vessel ranging in size from a small boat to a large ship tasked to carry military dispatches from ship to ship, from ship to shore, or, occasionally, from shore to shore.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="displacement" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Displacement_(ship)" title="Displacement (ship)">displacement</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The weight of water displaced by the immersed volume of a ship's hull, exactly equivalent to the weight of the whole ship.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="displacement_hull" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">displacement <a href="/wiki/Hull_(watercraft)" title="Hull (watercraft)">hull</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A hull designed to travel through the water, rather than planing over it.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="disposable_ship" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Disposable_ship" title="Disposable ship">disposable ship</a></dfn></dt>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also <b><a href="#drogher"><span title="See entry on this page at § drogher" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">drogher</span></a></b>, <b>raft ship</b>, <b>timber drogher</b>, or <b>timber ship</b>.</span></p>
<dd class="glossary">A barely seaworthy ship of the 19th century assembled from large timbers lashed or pegged together and designed to make a single voyage from North America to the United Kingdom and then to be disassembled so that her timbers could be sold, thus avoiding high British taxes on lumber imported as cargo. When British taxes on imported lumber fell, the construction of disposable ships ceased.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="disrate" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">disrate</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To reduce in rank or rating; to demote.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="distinguishing_mark" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Distinguishing_mark" class="mw-redirect" title="Distinguishing mark">distinguishing mark</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A flag flown to distinguish ships of one seagoing service of a given country from ships of the country<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s other seagoing service(s) when ships of more than one of the country<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s seagoing services fly the same <a href="#ensign"><span title="See entry on this page at § ensign" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">ensign</span></a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="ditty_bag" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">ditty bag</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Bag or box for personal items.<sup id="cite_ref-NHHC_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHHC-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="division" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Division_(naval)" title="Division (naval)">division</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;Especially in the late 19th and early 20th centuries, a permanent battle formation of a fleet, often smaller than a squadron, equipped and trained to operate as a tactical unit under the overall command of a higher command, such as a fleet or squadron.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;Especially in modern usage, an administrative naval command, smaller than a squadron and often subordinate to an administrative squadron, responsible for the manning, training, supply, and maintenance of a group of ships or submarines but not for directing their operations at sea.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="divisional_transport_officer" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Divisional_Transport_Officer" class="mw-redirect" title="Divisional Transport Officer">Divisional Transport Officer</a></dfn></dt>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also <b>Divisional Naval Transport Officer</b></span></p>
<dd class="glossary">In British usage, a shore-based naval officer responsible for the efficient working of the <span title="See entry on this page at § transport" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#transport" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">transport</a></span>s and boats of the <a href="#flotilla"><span title="See entry on this page at § flotilla" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">flotilla</span></a>, <a href="#division"><span title="See entry on this page at § division" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">division</span></a>, or <span title="See entry on this page at § squadron" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#squadron" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">squadron</a></span> under his charge.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dock" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dock_(maritime)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dock (maritime)">dock</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;In American usage, a fixed structure attached to shore to which a vessel is secured when in port, generally synonymous with <i>pier</i> and <i>wharf</i>, except that <i>pier</i> tends to refer to structures used for tying up commercial ships and to structures extending from shore for use in fishing, while <i>dock</i> refers more generally to facilities used for tying up ships or boats, including recreational craft.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;In British usage, the body of water between two piers or wharves that accommodates vessels tied up at the piers or wharves.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">3.&#160;&#160;To tie up along a pier or wharf.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dockyard" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dockyard" class="mw-redirect" title="Dockyard">dockyard</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A facility where ships or boats are built and repaired. Routinely used as a synonym for <i>shipyard</i>, although <i>dockyard</i> is sometimes associated more closely with a facility used for maintenance and basing activities, while <i>shipyard</i> sometimes is associated more closely with a facility used in construction.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dodger" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">dodger</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A hood forward of a hatch or cockpit to protect the crew from wind and spray. Can be soft or hard.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dog" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dog_(engineering)#Door_dog" title="Dog (engineering)">dog</a></dfn></dt>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also <b>door dog</b> and <b>hatch dog</b>.</span></p>
<dd class="glossary">Device to secure doors and hatches. Typically used for watertight openings, but can apply elsewhere. "Dogging the hatches" is a common phrase.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dog_watch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dog_watch" title="Dog watch">dog watch</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A short <a href="/wiki/Watch_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Watch system">watch</a> period, generally half the usual time (e.g., a two-hour watch rather than a four-hour one). Such watches might be included in order to rotate the system over different days for fairness, or to allow both watches to eat their meals at approximately normal times.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="doghouse" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">doghouse</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A slang term (in the US, mostly) for a raised portion of a ship's deck. A doghouse is usually added to improve headroom below or to shelter a hatch.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dogvane" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">dogvane</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small <a href="/wiki/Weather_vane" title="Weather vane">weather vane</a>, sometimes improvised with a scrap of cloth, yarn, or other light material mounted within sight of the <a href="#helmsman"><span title="See entry on this page at § helmsman" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">helmsman</span></a>. See <i><span title="See entry on this page at § tell-tale" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#tell-tale" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">tell-tale</a></span></i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="doldrums" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Doldrums" class="mw-redirect" title="Doldrums">doldrums</a></dfn></dt>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also <b>equatorial calms</b>.</span></p>
<dd class="glossary">The equatorial trough, with special reference to the light and variable nature of the winds generally encountered there.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dolly_winch" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">dolly winch</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small winch mounted on the <span title="See entry on this page at § windlass" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#windlass" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">windlass</a></span>, used as an alternative to the <a href="#brail"><span title="See entry on this page at § brail" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">brails</span></a> winch when that is obstructed in some way (e.g. by deck cargo).<sup id="cite_ref-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary_2-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thames_Sailing_Barge_glossary-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dolphin" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dolphin_(structure)" title="Dolphin (structure)">dolphin</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A structure consisting of a number of piles driven into the seabed or riverbed as a marker.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dolphin_striker" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">dolphin striker</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A spar protruding vertically beneath a <a href="/wiki/Bowsprit" title="Bowsprit">bowsprit</a>, usually attached to the boswprit cap, used provide a mechanically advantageous run for the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Martingale_stay&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Martingale stay (page does not exist)">martingale stay</a>, and other ropes of a ship's rigging.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="donkey_engine" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Donkey_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Donkey engine">donkey engine</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A small auxiliary engine used either to start a larger engine or independently, e.g. for pumping water on steamships.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="donkeyman" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">donkeyman</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">One of a ship's engineering crew. Often a crewman responsible for maintaining a <a href="/wiki/Steam_donkey" title="Steam donkey">steam donkey</a>, or any machinery other than the main engines. On some ships, the Petty Officer in charge of engineroom ratings.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dorade_box" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dorade_box" title="Dorade box">dorade box</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A dorade box (also called a dorade vent, collector box, or simply a "ventilator") is a type of vent that permits the passage of air in and out of the cabin or engine room of a boat while keeping rain, spray, and sea wash out.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dory" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dory" title="Dory">dory</a></dfn></dt><span class="anchor" id="dorey"></span><span class="anchor" id="dori"></span><span class="anchor" id="doree"></span>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also <b>doree</b>, <b>dori</b>, or (Royal Navy) <b>dorey</b>.</span></p>
<dd class="glossary">A shallow-draft, lightweight boat, about 5 to 7 metres (16 to 23&#160;ft) long, with high sides, a flat bottom, and sharp <a href="#bow"><span title="See entry on this page at § bow" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">bows</span></a>. Traditionally used as fishing boats, both in coastal waters and in the open sea.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="double-banked" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">double-banked</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">(of the arrangement of oars on a boat) having two oarsmen seated on each <span title="See entry on this page at § thwart" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#thwart" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">thwart</a></span>, each of whom operates one oar on their side of the boat. This contrasts with <span title="See entry on this page at § single-banked" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#single-banked" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">single-banked</a></span>, where only one oarsman is seated on each thwart operating one oar on one side of the boat, with the oars alternating between port and starboard along the length of the boat. A third arrangement is to have one rower on each thwart working two oars, one on each side of the boat.<sup id="cite_ref-McKee_1983_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKee_1983-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 135">&#58;&#8202;135&#8202;</span></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="double-shotted" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">double-shotted</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The practice of loading smoothbore cannon with two cannonballs.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="doubling_the_angle_on_the_bow" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">doubling the angle on the bow</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A technique for establishing the distance from a point on land, such as a headland that is being passed. This is a type of <span title="See entry on this page at § running bearing" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#running_bearing" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">running bearing</a></span> which requires no plotting on the chart. The ship is sailed on a constant course and speed. The distance shown on the log is noted when the <span title="See entry on this page at § relative bearing" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#relative_bearing" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">relative bearing</a></span> of a fixed point is taken, and the increase in that bearing is watched until it is twice the original bearing, and the log is read again. The distance travelled between the two bearings is the distance of the ship from the fixed point when the second bearing was taken. Allowances for tidal streams may or may not be allowed for, depending on the accuracy required.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dover_cliffs" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">Dover cliffs</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A slang term for very rough seas with large white-capped waves.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="downbound" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">downbound</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;Travel downstream, with a following current.<sup id="cite_ref-USCG_NavRules_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USCG_NavRules-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;Eastward travel in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a> region (terminology used by the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Lawrence_Seaway_Development_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Lawrence Seaway Development Corporation">Saint Lawrence Seaway Development Corporation</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-SLSDC_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SLSDC-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup></dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="downflooding" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">downflooding</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The entry of water through any opening into the <a href="#hull"><span title="See entry on this page at § hull" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">hull</span></a> or <span title="See entry on this page at § superstructure" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#superstructure" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">superstructure</a></span> of an undamaged vessel, such as an open door or <span title="See entry on this page at § porthole" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#porthole" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">porthole</a></span>, loose or open hatch, ventilator opening, etc. Downflooding can occur due to a ship′s <span title="See entry on this page at § trim" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#trim" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">trim</a></span>, if she <a href="#heel"><span title="See entry on this page at § heel" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">heel</span></a>s or <a href="#list"><span title="See entry on this page at § list" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">list</span></a>s,  or if she becomes totally or partially submerged.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="downhaul" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Downhaul" title="Downhaul">downhaul</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A line used to control either a mobile <span title="See entry on this page at § spar" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#spar" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">spar</a></span>, or the shape of a sail. A downhaul can also be used to retrieve a sail back on deck.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="drabbler" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">drabbler</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">An extra strip of canvas secured below a <a href="#bonnet"><span title="See entry on this page at § bonnet" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">bonnet</span></a>, further to increase the area of a course.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="draft" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Draft_(hull)" title="Draft (hull)">draft</a></dfn></dt><span class="anchor" id="draught"></span>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also <b>draught</b>.</span></p>
<dd class="glossary">The depth of a ship's <a href="/wiki/Keel" title="Keel">keel</a> below the <span title="See entry on this page at § waterline" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#waterline" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">waterline</a></span>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dragon_boat" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dragon_boat" title="Dragon boat">dragon boat</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">One of a family of traditional paddled long boats of various designs and sizes found throughout Asia, Africa, and the Pacific islands. For competitive events, they are generally rigged with decorative Chinese dragon heads and tails. Dragon boat races are traditionally held during the annual summer solstice festival.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dreadnought" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dreadnought" title="Dreadnought">dreadnought</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of <a href="#battleship"><span title="See entry on this page at § battleship" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">battleship</span></a> designed with an "all-big-gun" armament layout in which the ship<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">&#39;</span>s primary gun power resided in a primary battery of its largest guns intended for use at long range, with other gun armament limited to small weapons intended for close-range defense against <a href="/wiki/Torpedo_boat" title="Torpedo boat">torpedo boats</a> and other small warships. Most, but not all, dreadnoughts also had steam turbine propulsion. Predominant from 1906, dreadnoughts differed from earlier steam battleships, retroactively dubbed <i><a href="/wiki/Pre-dreadnought_battleship" title="Pre-dreadnought battleship">predreadnoughts</a></i>, which had only a few large guns, relied on an intermediate secondary battery used at shorter ranges for most of their offensive power, and had <a href="/wiki/Triple-expansion_steam_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Triple-expansion steam engine">triple-expansion steam engines</a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dress_overall" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dress_overall" class="mw-redirect" title="Dress overall">dress overall</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">To string <a href="/wiki/International_Code_of_Signals" title="International Code of Signals">International Code of Signals</a> flags, arranged at random, from <span title="See entry on this page at § stemhead" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#stemhead" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">stemhead</a></span> to <span title="See entry on this page at § masthead" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#masthead" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">masthead</a></span>, between mastheads (if the vessel has more than one mast), and then down to the <span title="See entry on this page at § taffrail" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#taffrail" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">taffrail</a></span>, on a ship in harbor as a sign of celebration of a national, local, or personal anniversary, event, holiday, or occasion. When a ship is properly dressed overall, <a href="#ensign"><span title="See entry on this page at § ensign" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">ensigns</span></a> fly at each masthead unless displaced by another flag (e.g. that of a <a href="#flag_officer"><span title="See entry on this page at § flag officer" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">flag officer</span></a> on board), in addition to the ensign flown in the usual position at the <span title="See entry on this page at § stern" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#stern" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">stern</a></span>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dressing_down" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">dressing down</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;Treating old sails with oil or wax to renew them.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;A verbal reprimand.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dressing_lines" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">dressing lines</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Lines running from <span title="See entry on this page at § stemhead" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#stemhead" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">stemhead</a></span> to <span title="See entry on this page at § masthead" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#masthead" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">masthead</a></span>, between mastheads, and then down to the <span title="See entry on this page at § taffrail" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#taffrail" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">taffrail</a></span>, to which flags are attached when a ship is <a href="#dress_overall"><span title="See entry on this page at § dress overall" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">dressed overall</span></a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="drifter" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Drifter_(fishing_boat)" title="Drifter (fishing boat)">drifter</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A type of fishing boat designed to catch <a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">herring</a> in a long <a href="/wiki/Drift_net" class="mw-redirect" title="Drift net">drift net</a>, long used in the Netherlands and Great Britain.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="drink" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">drink</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Overboard and into the water (e.g. "it fell into the drink").</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="driver" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">driver</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The large sail flown from the <span title="See entry on this page at § mizzen" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#mizzen" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">mizzen</a></span> <a href="#gaff"><span title="See entry on this page at § gaff" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">gaff</span></a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="driver-mast" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">driver-mast</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">The fifth <span title="See entry on this page at § mast" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#mast" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">mast</a></span> of a six-masted <a href="#barquentine"><span title="See entry on this page at § barquentine" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">barquentine</span></a> or <a href="#gaff"><span title="See entry on this page at § gaff" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">gaff</span></a> <span title="See entry on this page at § schooner" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#schooner" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">schooner</a></span>. It is preceded by the <a href="#jigger_mast"><span title="See entry on this page at § jigger mast" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">jigger mast</span></a> and followed by the <span title="See entry on this page at § spanker mast" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#spanker_mast" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">spanker mast</a></span>. The sixth mast of the only seven-masted vessel, the gaff schooner <i><a href="/wiki/Thomas_W._Lawson_(ship)" title="Thomas W. Lawson (ship)">Thomas W. Lawson</a></i>, was normally called the pusher-mast.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="drogher" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">drogher</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">See <i><a href="#disposable_ship"><span title="See entry on this page at § disposable ship" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">disposable ship</span></a></i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="drogue" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Drogue" title="Drogue">drogue</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A device to slow a boat down in a storm so that it does not speed excessively down the slope of a wave and crash into the next one. It is generally constructed of heavy flexible material in the shape of a cone. See also <i><span title="See entry on this page at § sea anchor" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#sea_anchor" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">sea anchor</a></span></i>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="drudging" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Drudging&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Drudging (page does not exist)">drudging</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A technique of maintaining <span title="See entry on this page at § steerageway" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id=""><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_nautical_terms_(M-Z)#steerageway" title="Glossary of nautical terms (M-Z)">steerageway</a></span> when going downstream with neither engine nor wind to sail. The vessel uses its anchor to draw itself head-to-stream, then lifts the anchor and drifts stern-first downstream, ferry gliding to maintain position within the stream. As steerage begins to reduce, the vessel anchors again and then repeats the whole procedure as required.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="drydock" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Drydock" class="mw-redirect" title="Drydock">drydock</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">A narrow basin or vessel used for the construction, maintenance, and repair of ships, boats, and other watercraft that can be flooded to allow a load to be floated in, then drained to allow that load to come to rest on a dry platform.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="drying_harbour" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">drying harbour</dfn></dt>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also <b>drying mooring</b>.</span></p>
<dd class="glossary">A harbour where the water wholly or partly recedes as the tide goes out, leaving any vessel moored there <a href="#aground"><span title="See entry on this page at § aground" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">aground</span></a>.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dunnage" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary">dunnage</dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">1.&#160;&#160;Loose packing material used to protect a ship's cargo from damage during transport. See also <i><a href="#fardage"><span title="See entry on this page at § fardage" class="glossary-link-internal" style="border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;" id="">fardage</span></a></i>.</dd>
<dd class="glossary">2.&#160;&#160;Personal baggage.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dutch_barge" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/wiki/Dutch_barge" title="Dutch barge">Dutch barge</a></dfn></dt>
<p class="glossary-hatnote"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><span role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref">Also <b>schuyt</b>.</span></p>
<dd class="glossary">Any of several types of traditional flat-bottomed shoal-draught sailing barge, originally used for carrying cargo in the Zuyder Zee and on the rivers of the Netherlands.</dd>

<dt class="glossary" id="dutch_built" style="margin-top: 0.4em;"><dfn class="glossary"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dutch_built&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dutch built (page does not exist)">Dutch built</a></dfn></dt>
<dd class="glossary">Term of abuse implying shoddiness or (when directed at a person) stupidity or stubbornness, usually embellished with other oaths and insults tagged on fore and aft.</dd>
</dl>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1098327943"/>
